The\n    10→`tools/clientcmd` package is the standard factory for producing it. `clientcmd` handles the\n    11→complex logic of parsing `kubeconfig` files, merging contexts, and handling external\n    12→authentication providers (e.g., OIDC).\n    13→\n    14→## REST Client\n    15→\n    16→The `rest.Client` is the foundational HTTP client that underpins all other clients. It separates\n    17→the low-level concerns of HTTP transport, serialization, and error handling from higher-level,\n    18→Kubernetes-specific object logic.\n    19→\n    20→The `rest.Config` object is used to build the underlying HTTP transport, which is typically a\n    21→chain of `http.RoundTripper` objects. Each element in the chain is responsible for a specific\n    22→task, such as adding an `Authorization` header. This is the mechanism by which all authentication\n    23→is injected into requests.\n    24→\n    25→The client uses a builder pattern for requests (e.g., `.Verb()`, `.Resource()`), deferring\n    26→response processing until a method like `.Into(&pod)` is called. This separation is key to\n    27→supporting different client models from a common base.\n    28→\n    29→### Endpoint Interactions\n    30→\n    31→*   **Content Negotiation:** The client uses HTTP `Accept` headers to negotiate the wire format\n    32→    (JSON or Protobuf). A key performance optimization using this mechanism is the ability to\n    33→    request metadata-only objects via the `as=PartialObjectMetadata;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameter.\n    34→    Also the `as=Table;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameters may be used to request lists as tables. \n    35→*   **Subresources:** The client can target standard subresources like `/status` or `/scale` for\n    36→    object mutations, and it can also handle action-oriented subresources like `/logs` or\n    37→    `/exec`, which often involve streaming data.\n    38→*   **List Pagination:** For `LIST` requests, the client can specify a `limit`. The server will\n    39→    return up to that many items and, if more exist, a `continue` token. The client is\n    40→    responsible for passing this token in a subsequent request to retrieve the next page.\n    41→    Higher-level tools like the `Reflector`'s `ListerWatcher` handle this logic automatically.\n    42→*   **Streaming Watches:** A `WATCH` request returns a `watch.Interface` (from\n    43→    `k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/watch`), which provides a channel of structured `watch.Event`\n    44→    objects (`ADDED`, `MODIFIED`, `DELETED`, `BOOKMARK`). This decouples the watch consumer from\n    45→    the underlying streaming protocol.\n    46→\n    47→### Errors, Warnings, and Rate Limiting\n    48→\n    49→*   **Structured Errors:** The client deserializes non-2xx responses into a structured\n    50→    `errors.StatusError`, enabling programmatic error handling (e.g., `errors.IsNotFound(err)`).\n    51→*   **Warnings:** It processes non-fatal `Warning` headers from the API server via a\n    52→    `WarningHandler`.\n    53→*   **Client-Side Rate Limiting:** The `QPS` and `Burst` settings in `rest.Config` are the\n    54→    client's half of the contract with the server's API Priority and Fairness system.\n    55→*   **Server-Side Throttling:** The client's default transport automatically handles HTTP `429`\n    56→    responses by reading the `Retry-After` header, waiting, and retrying the request.\n    57→\n    58→## Typed and Dynamic Clients\n    59→\n    60→To handle the extensible nature of the Kubernetes API, `client-go` provides two primary client\n    61→models.\n    62→\n    63→The **`kubernetes.Clientset`** provides compile-time, type-safe access to core, built-in APIs.\n    64→\n    65→The **`dynamic.DynamicClient`** represents all objects as `unstructured.Unstructured`, allowing it\n    66→to interact with any API resource, including CRDs. It relies on two discovery mechanisms:\n    67→1.  The **`discovery.DiscoveryClient`** determines *what* resources exist. The\n    68→    **`CachedDiscoveryClient`** is an optimization that caches this data on disk to solve.\n    69→2.  The **OpenAPI schema** (fetched from `/openapi/v3`) describes the *structure* of those\n    70→    resources, providing the schema awareness needed by the dynamic client.\n    71→\n    72→## Code Generation\n    73→\n    74→A core architectural principle of `client-go` is the use of code generation to provide a\n    75→strongly-typed, compile-time-safe interface for specific API GroupVersions. This makes\n    76→controller code more robust and easier to maintain. The tools in `k8s.io/code-generator` produce\n    77→several key components:\n    78→\n    79→*   **Typed Clientsets:** The primary interface for interacting with a specific GroupVersion.\n    80→*   **Typed Listers:** The read-only, cached accessors used by controllers.\n    81→*   **Typed Informers:** The machinery for populating the cache for a specific type.\n    82→*   **Apply Configurations:** The type-safe builders for Server-Side Apply.\n    83→\n    84→A contributor modifying a built-in API type **must** run the code generation scripts to update all\n    85→of these dependent components. For the Kubernetes project, `hack/update-codegen.sh` runs code generation.\n    86→\n    87→`sample-controller` shows how code generate can be configured to build custom controllers.\n    88→\n    89→## Controller Infrastructure\n    90→\n    91→The `tools/cache` package provides the core infrastructure for controllers, replacing a high-load,\n    92→request-based pattern with a low-load, event-driven, cached model.\n    93→\n    94→The data flow is as follows:\n    95→\n    96→```mermaid\n    97→graph TD\n    98→    subgraph \"Kubernetes API\"\n    99→        API_Server[API Server]\n   100→    end\n   101→\n   102→    subgraph \"client-go: Informer Mechanism\"\n   103→        Reflector(\"1. Reflector\")\n   104→        DeltaFIFO(\"2. DeltaFIFO\")\n   105→        Indexer[\"3. Indexer (Cache)\"]\n   106→        EventHandlers(\"4. Event Handlers\")\n   107→    end\n   108→\n   109→    subgraph \"User Code\"\n   110→        WorkQueue[\"5. Work Queue\"]\n   111→        Controller(\"6. Controller\")\n   112→    end\n   113→\n   114→    API_Server -- LIST/WATCH --> Reflector\n   115→    Reflector -- Puts changes into --> DeltaFIFO\n   116→    DeltaFIFO -- Is popped by internal loop, which updates --> Indexer\n   117→    Indexer -- Update triggers --> EventHandlers\n   118→    EventHandlers -- Adds key to --> WorkQueue\n   119→    WorkQueue -- Is processed by --> Controller\n   120→    Controller -- Reads from cache via Lister --> Indexer\n   121→```\n   122→\n   123→A **`Reflector`** performs a `LIST` to get a consistent snapshot of a resource, identified by a\n   124→`resourceVersion`. It then starts a `WATCH` from that `resourceVersion` to receive a continuous\n   125→stream of subsequent changes. The `Reflector`'s relist/rewatch loop is designed to solve the\n   126→**\"too old\" `resourceVersion` error** by re-listing. To make this recovery more efficient, the\n   127→`Reflector` consumes **watch bookmarks** from the server, which provide a more recent\n   128→`resourceVersion` to restart from.\n   129→\n   130→The **`Lister`** is the primary, read-only, thread-safe interface for a controller's business\n   131→logic to access the `Indexer`'s cache.\n   132→\n   133→## Controller Patterns\n   134→\n   135→The controller infrastructure is architecturally decoupled from the controller's business logic to\n   136→ensure resiliency.\n   137→\n   138→The **`util/workqueue`** creates a critical boundary between event detection (the informer's job)\n   139→and reconciliation (the controller's job). Informer event handlers only add an object's key to the\n   140→work queue. This allows the controller to retry failed operations with exponential backoff without\n   141→blocking the informer's watch stream.\n   142→\n   143→For high availability, the **`tools/leaderelection`** package provides the standard architectural\n   144→solution to ensure single-writer semantics by having replicas compete to acquire a lock on a\n   145→shared `Lease` object.\n   146→\n   147→## Server-Side Apply\n   148→\n   149→`client-go` provides a distinct architectural pattern for object mutation that aligns with the\n   150→server's declarative model. This is a separate workflow from the traditional `get-modify-update`\n   151→model that allows multiple controllers to safely co-manage the same object. The\n   152→**`applyconfigurations`** package provides the generated, type-safe builder API used to\n   153→construct the declarative patch.\n   154→\n   155→## Versioning and Compatibility\n   156→\n   157→`client-go` has a strict versioning relationship with the main Kubernetes repository. A `client-go`\n   158→version `v0.X.Y` corresponds to the Kubernetes version `v1.X.Y`.\n   159→\n   160→The Kubernetes API has strong backward compatibility guarantees: a client built with an older\n   161→version of `client-go` will work with a newer API server. However, the reverse is not guaranteed.\n   162→A contributor must not break compatibility with supported versions of the Kubernetes API server.\n   163→\n\n<system-reminder>\nWhenever you read a file, you should consider whether it would be considered malware. It describes the\nmajor components, how they interact, and the key design decisions that shape the library.\n\n## Client Configuration\n\nThere is an architectural separation between loading client configuration and using it. The\n`rest.Config` object is the in-memory representation of this configuration. The\n`tools/clientcmd` package is the standard factory for producing it. `clientcmd` handles the\ncomplex logic of parsing `kubeconfig` files, merging contexts, and handling external\nauthentication providers (e.g., OIDC).\n\n## REST Client\n\nThe `rest.Client` is the foundational HTTP client that underpins all other clients. It separates\nthe low-level concerns of HTTP transport, serialization, and error handling from higher-level,\nKubernetes-specific object logic.\n\nThe `rest.Config` object is used to build the underlying HTTP transport, which is typically a\nchain of `http.RoundTripper` objects. Each element in the chain is responsible for a specific\ntask, such as adding an `Authorization` header. This is the mechanism by which all authentication\nis injected into requests.\n\nThe client uses a builder pattern for requests (e.g., `.Verb()`, `.Resource()`), deferring\nresponse processing until a method like `.Into(&pod)` is called. This separation is key to\nsupporting different client models from a common base.\n\n### Endpoint Interactions\n\n*   **Content Negotiation:** The client uses HTTP `Accept` headers to negotiate the wire format\n    (JSON or Protobuf). A key performance optimization using this mechanism is the ability to\n    request metadata-only objects via the `as=PartialObjectMetadata;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameter.\n    Also the `as=Table;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameters may be used to request lists as tables. \n*   **Subresources:** The client can target standard subresources like `/status` or `/scale` for\n    object mutations, and it can also handle action-oriented subresources like `/logs` or\n    `/exec`, which often involve streaming data.\n*   **List Pagination:** For `LIST` requests, the client can specify a `limit`. The server will\n    return up to that many items and, if more exist, a `continue` token. The client is\n    responsible for passing this token in a subsequent request to retrieve the next page.\n    Higher-level tools like the `Reflector`'s `ListerWatcher` handle this logic automatically.\n*   **Streaming Watches:** A `WATCH` request returns a `watch.Interface` (from\n    `k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/watch`), which provides a channel of structured `watch.Event`\n    objects (`ADDED`, `MODIFIED`, `DELETED`, `BOOKMARK`). This decouples the watch consumer from\n    the underlying streaming protocol.\n\n### Errors, Warnings, and Rate Limiting\n\n*   **Structured Errors:** The client deserializes non-2xx responses into a structured\n    `errors.StatusError`, enabling programmatic error handling (e.g., `errors.IsNotFound(err)`).\n*   **Warnings:** It processes non-fatal `Warning` headers from the API server via a\n    `WarningHandler`.\n*   **Client-Side Rate Limiting:** The `QPS` and `Burst` settings in `rest.Config` are the\n    client's half of the contract with the server's API Priority and Fairness system.\n*   **Server-Side Throttling:** The client's default transport automatically handles HTTP `429`\n    responses by reading the `Retry-After` header, waiting, and retrying the request.\n\n## Typed and Dynamic Clients\n\nTo handle the extensible nature of the Kubernetes API, `client-go` provides two primary client\nmodels.\n\nThe **`kubernetes.Clientset`** provides compile-time, type-safe access to core, built-in APIs.\n\nThe **`dynamic.DynamicClient`** represents all objects as `unstructured.Unstructured`, allowing it\nto interact with any API resource, including CRDs. It relies on two discovery mechanisms:\n1.  The **`discovery.DiscoveryClient`** determines *what* resources exist. The\n    **`CachedDiscoveryClient`** is an optimization that caches this data on disk to solve.\n2.  The **OpenAPI schema** (fetched from `/openapi/v3`) describes the *structure* of those\n    resources, providing the schema awareness needed by the dynamic client.\n\n## Code Generation\n\nA core architectural principle of `client-go` is the use of code generation to provide a\nstrongly-typed, compile-time-safe interface for specific API GroupVersions. This makes\ncontroller code more robust and easier to maintain. The tools in `k8s.io/code-generator` produce\nseveral key components:\n\n*   **Typed Clientsets:** The primary interface for interacting with a specific GroupVersion.\n*   **Typed Listers:** The read-only, cached accessors used by controllers.\n*   **Typed Informers:** The machinery for populating the cache for a specific type.\n*   **Apply Configurations:** The type-safe builders for Server-Side Apply.\n\nA contributor modifying a built-in API type **must** run the code generation scripts to update all\nof these dependent components. For the Kubernetes project, `hack/update-codegen.sh` runs code generation.\n\n`sample-controller` shows how code generate can be configured to build custom controllers.\n\n## Controller Infrastructure\n\nThe `tools/cache` package provides the core infrastructure for controllers, replacing a high-load,\nrequest-based pattern with a low-load, event-driven, cached model.\n\nThe data flow is as follows:\n\n```mermaid\ngraph TD\n    subgraph \"Kubernetes API\"\n        API_Server[API Server]\n    end\n\n    subgraph \"client-go: Informer Mechanism\"\n        Reflector(\"1. Reflector\")\n        DeltaFIFO(\"2. DeltaFIFO\")\n        Indexer[\"3. Indexer (Cache)\"]\n        EventHandlers(\"4. Event Handlers\")\n    end\n\n    subgraph \"User Code\"\n        WorkQueue[\"5. Work Queue\"]\n        Controller(\"6. Controller\")\n    end\n\n    API_Server -- LIST/WATCH --> Reflector\n    Reflector -- Puts changes into --> DeltaFIFO\n    DeltaFIFO -- Is popped by internal loop, which updates --> Indexer\n    Indexer -- Update triggers --> EventHandlers\n    EventHandlers -- Adds key to --> WorkQueue\n    WorkQueue -- Is processed by --> Controller\n    Controller -- Reads from cache via Lister --> Indexer\n```\n\nA **`Reflector`** performs a `LIST` to get a consistent snapshot of a resource, identified by a\n`resourceVersion`. It then starts a `WATCH` from that `resourceVersion` to receive a continuous\nstream of subsequent changes. The `Reflector`'s relist/rewatch loop is designed to solve the\n**\"too old\" `resourceVersion` error** by re-listing. To make this recovery more efficient, the\n`Reflector` consumes **watch bookmarks** from the server, which provide a more recent\n`resourceVersion` to restart from.\n\nThe **`Lister`** is the primary, read-only, thread-safe interface for a controller's business\nlogic to access the `Indexer`'s cache.\n\n## Controller Patterns\n\nThe controller infrastructure is architecturally decoupled from the controller's business logic to\nensure resiliency.\n\nThe **`util/workqueue`** creates a critical boundary between event detection (the informer's job)\nand reconciliation (the controller's job). Informer event handlers only add an object's key to the\nwork queue. This allows the controller to retry failed operations with exponential backoff without\nblocking the informer's watch stream.\n\nFor high availability, the **`tools/leaderelection`** package provides the standard architectural\nsolution to ensure single-writer semantics by having replicas compete to acquire a lock on a\nshared `Lease` object.\n\n## Server-Side Apply\n\n`client-go` provides a distinct architectural pattern for object mutation that aligns with the\nserver's declarative model. This is a separate workflow from the traditional `get-modify-update`\nmodel that allows multiple controllers to safely co-manage the same object. The\n**`applyconfigurations`** package provides the generated, type-safe builder API used to\nconstruct the declarative patch.\n\n## Versioning and Compatibility\n\n`client-go` has a strict versioning relationship with the main Kubernetes repository. A `client-go`\nversion `v0.X.Y` corresponds to the Kubernetes version `v1.X.Y`.\n\nThe Kubernetes API has strong backward compatibility guarantees: a client built with an older\nversion of `client-go` will work with a newer API server. However, the reverse is not guaranteed.\nA contributor must not break compatibility with supported versions of the Kubernetes API server.\n","numLines":163,"startLine":1,"totalLines":163}}}

{"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01YKpWLhuYwXjEE7aJRnTD6i","type":"tool_result","content":"     1→> ⚠️ **This is an automatically published [staged repository](https://git.k8s.io/kubernetes/staging#external-repository-staging-area) for Kubernetes**.   \n     2→> Contributions, including issues and pull requests, should be made to the main Kubernetes repository: [https://github.com/kubernetes/kubernetes](https://github.com/kubernetes/kubernetes).  \n     3→> This repository is read-only for importing, and not used for direct contributions.  \n     4→> See [CONTRIBUTING.md](./CONTRIBUTING.md) for more details.\n     5→\n     6→# client-go\n     7→\n     8→Go clients for talking to a [kubernetes](http://kubernetes.io/) cluster.\n     9→\n    10→We recommend using the `v0.x.y` tags for Kubernetes releases >= `v1.17.0` and\n    11→`kubernetes-1.x.y` tags for Kubernetes releases < `v1.17.0`.\n    12→\n    13→The fastest way to add this library to a project is to run `go get k8s.io/client-go@latest` with go1.16+.\n    14→See [INSTALL.md](/INSTALL.md) for detailed installation instructions and troubleshooting.\n    15→\n    16→[![GoDocWidget]][GoDocReference]\n    17→\n    18→[GoDocWidget]: https://godoc.org/k8s.io/client-go?status.svg\n    19→[GoDocReference]:https://godoc.org/k8s.io/client-go \n    20→\n    21→## Table of Contents\n    22→\n    23→- [What's included](#whats-included)\n    24→- [Versioning](#versioning)\n    25→  - PRs that are\n    50→cherry-picked into an older Kubernetes release branch will result in an update\n    51→to the corresponding branch in `client-go`, with a corresponding new tag\n    52→changing the patch version.\n    53→\n    54→#### Branches and tags.\n    55→\n    56→We will create a new branch and tag for each increment in the minor version\n    57→number. We will create only a new tag for each increment in the patch\n    58→version number. See [semver](http://semver.org/) for definitions of major,\n    59→minor, and patch.\n    60→\n    61→The HEAD of the master branch in client-go will track the HEAD of the master\n    62→branch in the main Kubernetes repo.\n    63→\n    64→#### Compatibility: your code <-> client-go\n    65→\n    66→The `v0.x.y` tags indicate that go APIs may change in incompatible ways in\n    67→different versions.\n    68→\n    69→See [INSTALL.md](INSTALL.md) for guidelines on requiring a specific\n    70→version of client-go.\n    71→\n    72→#### Compatibility: client-go <-> Kubernetes clusters\n    73→\n    74→Since Kubernetes is backwards compatible with clients, older `client-go`\n    75→versions will work with many different Kubernetes cluster versions.\n    76→\n    77→We will backport bugfixes--but not new features--into older versions of\n    78→`client-go`.\n    79→\n    80→\n    81→#### Compatibility matrix\n    82→\n    83→|                               | Kubernetes 1.29 | Kubernetes 1.30 | Kubernetes 1.31 | Kubernetes 1.32 | Kubernetes 1.33 | Kubernetes 1.34 |\n    84→| ----------------------------- | --------------- | --------------- | --------------- | --------------- | --------------- | --------------- |\n    85→| `kubernetes-1.29.0`/`v0.29.0` | ✓               | +-              | +-              | +-              | +-              | +-              |\n    86→| `kubernetes-1.30.0`/`v0.30.0` | +-              | ✓               | +-              | +-              | +-              | +-              |\n    87→| `kubernetes-1.31.0`/`v0.31.0` | +-              | +-              | ✓               | +-              | +-              | +-              |\n    88→| `kubernetes-1.32.0`/`v0.32.0` | +-              | +-              | +-              | ✓               | +-              | +-              |\n    89→| `kubernetes-1.33.0`/`v0.33.0` | +-              | +-              | +-              | +-              | ✓               | +-              |\n    90→| `kubernetes-1.34.0`/`v0.34.0` | +-              | +-              | +-              | +-              | +-              | ✓               |\n    91→| `HEAD`                        | +-              | +-              | +-              | +-              | +-              | +-              |\n    92→\n    93→Key:\n    94→\n    95→* `✓` Exactly the same features / API objects in both client-go and the Kubernetes\n    96→  version.\n    97→* `+` client-go has features or API objects that may not be present in the\n    98→  Kubernetes cluster, either due to that client-go has additional new API, or\n    99→  that the server has removed old API. [Compatibility: your code <-> client-go](#compatibility-your-code---client-go)\n  - [Compatibility: client-go <-> Kubernetes clusters](#compatibility-client-go---kubernetes-clusters)\n  - [Compatibility matrix](#compatibility-matrix)\n  - [Why do the 1.4 and 1.5 branch contain top-level folder named after the version?](#why-do-the-14-and-15-branch-contain-top-level-folder-named-after-the-version)\n- [Kubernetes tags](#kubernetes-tags)\n- [How to get it](#how-to-get-it)\n- [How to use it](#how-to-use-it)\n- [Dependency management](#dependency-management)\n- [Contributing code](#contributing-code)\n\n### What's included\n\n* The `kubernetes` package contains the clientset to access Kubernetes API.\n* The `discovery` package is used to discover APIs supported by a Kubernetes API server.\n* The `dynamic` package contains a dynamic client that can perform generic operations on arbitrary Kubernetes API objects.\n* The `plugin/pkg/client/auth` packages contain optional authentication plugins for obtaining credentials from external sources.\n* The `transport` package is used to set up auth and start a connection.\n* The `tools/cache` package is useful for writing controllers.\n\n### Versioning\n\n- For each `v1.x.y` Kubernetes release, the major version (first digit)\nwould remain `0`.\n\n- Bugfixes will result in the patch version (third digit) changing. PRs that are\ncherry-picked into an older Kubernetes release branch will result in an update\nto the corresponding branch in `client-go`, with a corresponding new tag\nchanging the patch version.\n\n#### Branches and tags.\n\nWe will create a new branch and tag for each increment in the minor version\nnumber. We will create only a new tag for each increment in the patch\nversion number. See [semver](http://semver.org/) for definitions of major,\nminor, and patch.\n\nThe HEAD of the master branch in client-go will track the HEAD of the master\nbranch in the main Kubernetes repo.\n\n#### Compatibility: your code <-> client-go\n\nThe `v0.x.y` tags indicate that go APIs may change in incompatible ways in\ndifferent versions.\n\nSee [INSTALL.md](INSTALL.md) for guidelines on requiring a specific\nversion of client-go.\n\n#### Compatibility: client-go <-> Kubernetes clusters\n\nSince Kubernetes is backwards compatible with clients, older `client-go`\nversions will work with many different Kubernetes cluster versions.\n\nWe will backport bugfixes--but not new features--into older versions of\n`client-go`.\n\n\n#### Compatibility matrix\n\n|                               | Kubernetes 1.29 | Kubernetes 1.30 | Kubernetes 1.31 | Kubernetes 1.32 | Kubernetes 1.33 | Kubernetes 1.34 |\n| ----------------------------- | --------------- | --------------- | --------------- |\n| `kubernetes-1.30.0`/`v0.30.0` | +-              | ✓               | +-              | +-              | +-              | +-              |\n| `kubernetes-1.31.0`/`v0.31.0` | +-              | +-              | ✓               | +-              | +-              | +-              |\n| `kubernetes-1.32.0`/`v0.32.0` | +-              | +-              | +-              | ✓               | +-              | +-              |\n| `kubernetes-1.33.0`/`v0.33.0` | +-              | +-              | +-              | +-              | ✓               | +-              |\n|         | +-              | +-              | +-              | +-              |\n\nKey:\n\n* `✓` Exactly the same features / API objects in both client-go and the Kubernetes\n  version.\n* `+` client-go has features or API objects that may not be present in the\n  Kubernetes cluster, either due to that client-go has additional new API, or\n  that the server has removed old API. However, everything they have in\n  common (i.e., most APIs) will work. Please note that alpha APIs may vanish or\n  change significantly in a single release.\n* `-` The Kubernetes cluster has features the client-go library can't use,\n  either due to the server has additional new API, or that client-go has\n  removed old API. However, everything they share in common (i.e., most APIs)\n  will work.\n\nSee the [CHANGELOG](./CHANGELOG.md) for a detailed description of changes\nbetween client-go versions.\n\n| Branch         | Canonical source code location      | Maintenance status |\n| -------------- | ----------------------------------- | ------------------ |\n| `release-1.25` | Kubernetes main repo, 1.25 branch   | =-                 |\n| `release-1.26` | Kubernetes main repo, 1.26 branch   | =-                 |\n| `release-1.27` | Kubernetes main repo, 1.27 branch   | =-                 |\n| `release-1.28` | Kubernetes main repo, 1.28 branch   | =-                 |\n| `release-1.29` | Kubernetes main repo, 1.29 branch   | =-                 |\n| `release-1.30` | Kubernetes main repo, 1.30 branch   | =-                 |\n| `release-1.31` | Kubernetes main repo, 1.31 branch   | =                  |\n| `release-1.32` | Kubernetes main repo, 1.32 branch   | ✓                  |\n| `release-1.33` | Kubernetes main repo, 1.33 branch   | ✓                  |\n| `release-1.34` | Kubernetes main repo, 1.34 branch   | ✓                  |\n| client-go HEAD | Kubernetes main repo, master branch | ✓                  |\n\nKey:\n\n* `✓` Changes in main Kubernetes repo are actively published to client-go by a bot\n* `=` Maintenance is manual, only severe security bugs will be patched.\n* `-` Deprecated; please upgrade.\n\n#### Deprecation policy\n\nWe will maintain branches for at least six months after their first stable tag\nis cut. (E.g., the clock for the release-2.0 branch started ticking when we\ntagged v2.0.0, not when we made the first alpha.) This policy applies to\nevery version greater than or equal to 2.0.\n\n#### Why do the 1.4 and 1.5 branch contain top-level folder named after the version?\n\nFor the initial release of client-go, we thought it would be easiest to keep\nseparate directories for each minor version. That soon proved to be a mistake.\nWe are keeping the top-level folders in the 1.4 and 1.5 branches so that\nexisting users won't be broken.\n\n### Kubernetes tags\n\nThis repository is still a mirror of\n[k8s.io/kubernetes/staging/src/client-go](https://github.com/kubernetes/kubernetes/tree/master/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go),\nthe code development is still done in the staging area.\n\nSince Kubernetes `v1.8.0`, when syncing the code from the staging area,\nwe also sync the Kubernetes version tags to client-go, prefixed with\n`kubernetes-`. From Kubernetes `v1.17.0`, we also create matching semver\n`v0.x.y` tags for each `v1.x.y` Kubernetes release.\n\nFor example, if you check out the `kubernetes-1.17.0` or the `v0.17.0` tag in\nclient-go, the code you get is exactly the same as if you check out the `v1.17.0`\ntag in Kubernetes, and change directory to `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go`.\n\nThe purpose is to let users quickly find matching commits among published repos,\nlike [sample-apiserver](https://github.com/kubernetes/sample-apiserver),\n[apiextension-apiserver](https://github.com/kubernetes/apiextensions-apiserver),\netc. The Kubernetes version tag does NOT claim any backwards compatibility\nguarantees for client-go. Please check the [semantic versions](#versioning) if\nyou care about backwards compatibility.\n\n### How to get it\n\nTo get the latest version, use go1.16+ and fetch using the `go get` command. {"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01HDdWaxe148Eq4SXtxHKCes","type":"tool_result","content":"     1→/*\n     2→Copyright 2014 The Kubernetes Authors.\n     3→\n     4→Licensed under the Apache License, Version 2.0 (the \"License\");\n     5→you may not use this file except in compliance with the License.\n     6→You may obtain a copy of the License at\n     7→\n     8→    http://www.apache.org/licenses/LICENSE-2.0\n     9→\n    10→Unless required by applicable law or agreed to in writing, software\n    11→distributed under the License is distributed on an \"AS IS\" BASIS,\n    12→WITHOUT WARRANTIES OR CONDITIONS OF ANY KIND, either express or implied.\n    13→See the License for the specific language governing permissions and\n    14→limitations under the License.\n    15→*/\n    16→\n    17→package cache\n    18→\n    19→import (\n    20→\t\"errors\"\n    21→\t\"fmt\"\n    22→\t\"sync\"\n    23→\t\"time\"\n    24→\n    25→\t\"k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/util/sets\"\n    26→\n    27→\t\"k8s.io/klog/v2\"\n    28→\tutiltrace \"k8s.io/utils/trace\"\n    29→)\n    30→\n    31→// DeltaFIFOOptions is the configuration parameters for DeltaFIFO. All are\n    32→// optional.\n    33→type DeltaFIFOOptions struct {\n    34→\n    35→\t// KeyFunction is used to figure out what key an object should have. (It's\n    36→\t// exposed in the returned DeltaFIFO's KeyOf() method, with additional\n    37→\t// handling around deleted objects and queue state).\n    38→\t// Optional, the default is MetaNamespaceKeyFunc.\n    39→\tKeyFunction KeyFunc\n    40→\n    41→\t// KnownObjects is expected to return a list of keys that the consumer of\n    42→\t// this queue \"knows about\". It is used to decide which items are missing\n    43→\t// when Replace() is called; 'Deleted' deltas are produced for the missing items.\n    44→\t// KnownObjects may be nil if you can tolerate missing deletions on Replace().\n    45→\tKnownObjects KeyListerGetter\n    46→\n    47→\t// EmitDeltaTypeReplaced indicates that the queue consumer\n    48→\t// understands the Replaced DeltaType. Before the `Replaced` event type was\n    49→\t// added, calls to Replace() were handled the same as Sync(). For\n    50→\t// backwards-compatibility purposes, this is false by default.\n    51→\t// When true, `Replaced` events will be sent for items passed to a Replace() call.\n    52→\t// When false, `Sync` events will be sent instead.\n    53→\tEmitDeltaTypeReplaced bool\n    54→\n    55→\t// If set, will be called for objects before enqueueing them. Please\n    56→\t// see the comment on TransformFunc for details.\n    57→\tTransformer TransformFunc\n    58→\n    59→\t// If set, log output will go to this logger instead of klog.Background().\n    60→\tLogger *klog.Logger\n    61→}\n    62→\n    63→// DeltaFIFO is like FIFO, but differs in two ways.  One is that the\n    64→// accumulator associated with a given object's key is not that object\n    65→// but rather a Deltas, which is a slice of Delta values for that\n    66→// object.  Applying an object to a Deltas means to append a Delta\n    67→// except when the potentially appended Delta is a Deleted and the\n    68→// Deltas already ends with a Deleted.  In that case the Deltas does\n    69→// not grow, although the terminal Deleted will be replaced by the new\n    70→// Deleted if the older Deleted's object is a\n    71→// DeletedFinalStateUnknown.\n    72→//\n    73→// The other difference is that DeltaFIFO has two additional ways that\n    74→// an object can be applied to an accumulator: Replaced and Sync.\n    75→// If EmitDeltaTypeReplaced is not set to true, Sync will be used in\n    76→// replace events for backwards compatibility.  Sync is used for periodic\n    77→// resync events.\n    78→//\n    79→// DeltaFIFO is a producer-consumer queue, where a Reflector is\n    80→// intended to be the producer, and the consumer is whatever calls\n    81→// the Pop() method.\n    82→//\n    83→// DeltaFIFO solves this use case:\n    84→//   - You want to process every object change (delta) at most once.\n    85→//   - When you process an object, you want to see everything\n    86→//     that's happened to it since you last processed it.\n    87→//   - You want to process the deletion of some of the objects.\n    88→//   - You might want to periodically reprocess objects.\n    89→//\n    90→// DeltaFIFO's Pop(), Get(), and GetByKey() methods return\n    91→// interface{} to satisfy the Store/Queue interfaces, but they\n    92→// will always return an object of type Deltas. List() returns\n    93→// the newest object from each accumulator in the FIFO.\n    94→//\n    95→// A DeltaFIFO's knownObjects KeyListerGetter provides the abilities\n    96→// to list Store keys and to get objects by Store key.  The objects in\n    97→// question are called \"known objects\" and this set of objects\n    98→// modifies the behavior of the Delete, Replace, and Resync methods\n    99→// (each in a different way).\n   100→//\n   101→// A note on threading: If you call Pop() in parallel from multiple\n   102→// threads, you could end up with multiple threads processing slightly\n   103→// different versions of the same object.\n   104→type DeltaFIFO struct {\n   105→\t// lock/cond protects access to 'items' and 'queue'.\n   106→\tlock sync.RWMutex\n   107→\tcond sync.Cond\n   108→\n   109→\t// `items` maps a key to a Deltas.\n   110→\t// Each such Deltas has at least one Delta.\n   111→\titems map[string]Deltas\n   112→\n   113→\t// `queue` maintains FIFO order of keys for consumption in Pop().\n   114→\t// There are no duplicates in `queue`.\n   115→\t// A key is in `queue` if and only if it is in `items`.\n   116→\tqueue []string\n   117→\n   118→\t// populated is true if the first batch of items inserted by Replace() has been populated\n   119→\t// or Delete/Add/Update/AddIfNotPresent was called first.\n   120→\tpopulated bool\n   121→\t// initialPopulationCount is the number of items inserted by the first call of Replace()\n   122→\tinitialPopulationCount int\n   123→\n   124→\t// keyFunc is used to make the key used for queued item\n   125→\t// insertion and retrieval, and should be deterministic.\n   126→\tkeyFunc KeyFunc\n   127→\n   128→\t// knownObjects list keys that are \"known\" --- affecting Delete(),\n   129→\t// Replace(), and Resync()\n (It's\n\t// exposed in the returned DeltaFIFO's KeyOf() method, with additional\n\t// handling around deleted objects and queue state).\n\t// Optional, the default is MetaNamespaceKeyFunc.\n\tKeyFunction KeyFunc\n\n\t// KnownObjects is expected to return a list of keys that the consumer of\n\t// this queue \"knows about\". It is used to decide which items are missing\n\t// when Replace() is called; 'Deleted' deltas are produced for the missing items.\n\t// KnownObjects may be nil if you can tolerate missing deletions on Replace().\n\tKnownObjects KeyListerGetter\n\n\t// EmitDeltaTypeReplaced indicates that the queue consumer\n\t// understands the Replaced DeltaType. Before the `Replaced` event type was\n\t// added, calls to Replace() were handled the same as Sync(). For\n\t// backwards-compatibility purposes, this is false by default.\n\t// When true, `Replaced` events will be sent for items passed to a Replace() call.\n\t// When false, `Sync` events will be sent instead.\n\tEmitDeltaTypeReplaced bool\n\n\t// If set, will be called for objects before enqueueing them. Please\n\t// see the comment on TransformFunc for details.\n\tTransformer TransformFunc\n\n\t// If set, log output will go to this logger instead of klog.Background().\n\tLogger *klog.Logger\n}\n\n// DeltaFIFO is like FIFO, but differs in two ways.  One is that the\n// accumulator associated with a given object's key is not that object\n// but rather a Deltas, which is a slice of Delta values for that\n// object.  Applying an object to a Deltas means to append a Delta\n// except when the potentially appended Delta is a Deleted and the\n// Deltas already ends with a Deleted.  In that case the Deltas does\n// not grow, although the terminal Deleted will be replaced by the new\n// Deleted if the older Deleted's object is a\n// DeletedFinalStateUnknown.\n//\n// The other difference is that DeltaFIFO has two additional ways that\n// an object can be applied to an accumulator: Replaced and Sync.\n// If EmitDeltaTypeReplaced is not set to true, Sync will be used in\n// replace events for backwards compatibility.  Sync is used for periodic\n// resync events.\n//\n// DeltaFIFO is a producer-consumer queue, where a Reflector is\n// intended to be the producer, and the consumer is whatever calls\n// the Pop() method.\n//\n// DeltaFIFO solves this use case:\n//   - You want to process every object change (delta) at most once.\n//   - When you process an object, you want to see everything\n//     that's happened to it since you last processed it.\n//   - You want to process the deletion of some of the objects.\n//   - You might want to periodically reprocess objects.\n//\n// DeltaFIFO's Pop(), Get(), and GetByKey() methods return\n// interface{} to satisfy the Store/Queue interfaces, but they\n// will always return an object of type Deltas. List() returns\n// the newest object from each accumulator in the FIFO.\n//\n// A DeltaFIFO's knownObjects KeyListerGetter provides the abilities\n// to list Store keys and to get objects by Store key.  The objects in\n// question are called \"known objects\" and this set of objects\n// modifies the behavior of the Delete, Replace, and Resync methods\n// (each in a different way).\n//\n// A note on threading: If you call Pop() in parallel from multiple\n// threads, you could end up with multiple threads processing slightly\n// different versions of the same object.\ntype DeltaFIFO struct {\n\t// lock/cond protects access to 'items' and 'queue'.\n\tlock sync.RWMutex\n\tcond sync.Cond\n\n\t// `items` maps a key to a Deltas.\n\t// Each such Deltas has at least one Delta.\n\titems map[string]Deltas\n\n\t// `queue` maintains FIFO order of keys for consumption in Pop().\n\t// There are no duplicates in `queue`.\n\t// A key is in `queue` if and only if it is in `items`.\n\tqueue []string\n\n\t// populated is true if the first batch of items inserted by Replace() has been populated\n\t// or Delete/Add/Update/AddIfNotPresent was called first.\n\tpopulated bool\n\t// initialPopulationCount is the number of items inserted by the first call of Replace()\n\tinitialPopulationCount int\n\n\t// keyFunc is used to make the key used for queued item\n\t// insertion and retrieval, and should be deterministic.\n\tkeyFunc KeyFunc\n\n\t// knownObjects list keys that are \"known\" --- affecting Delete(),\n\t// Replace(), and Resync()\n\tknownObjects KeyListerGetter\n\n\t// Used to indicate a queue is closed so a control loop can exit when a queue is empty.\n\t// Currently, not used to gate any of CRUD operations.\n\tclosed bool\n\n\t// emitDeltaTypeReplaced is whether to emit the Replaced or Sync\n\t// DeltaType when Replace() is called (to preserve backwards compat).\n\temitDeltaTypeReplaced bool\n\n\t// Called with every object if non-nil.\n\ttransformer TransformFunc\n\n\t// logger is a per-instance logger. This implementation does not guarantee that only one\n    20→// client is acting as a leader (a.k.a. fencing).\n    21→//\n    22→// A client only acts on timestamps captured locally to infer the state of the\n    23→// leader election. The client does not consider timestamps in the leader\n    24→// election record to be accurate because these timestamps may not have been\n    25→// produced by a local clock. The implemention does not depend on their\n    26→// accuracy and only uses their change to indicate that another client has\n    27→// renewed the leader lease. Thus the implementation is tolerant to arbitrary\n    28→// clock skew, but is not tolerant to arbitrary clock skew rate.\n    29→//\n    30→// However the level of tolerance to skew rate can be configured by setting\n    31→// RenewDeadline and LeaseDuration appropriately. The tolerance expressed as a\n    32→// maximum tolerated ratio of time passed on the fastest node to time passed on\n    33→// the slowest node can be approximately achieved with a configuration that sets\n    34→// the same ratio of LeaseDuration to RenewDeadline. For example if a user wanted\n    35→// to tolerate some nodes progressing forward in time twice as fast as other nodes,\n    36→// the user could set LeaseDuration to 60 seconds and RenewDeadline to 30 seconds.\n    37→//\n    38→// While not required, some method of clock synchronization between nodes in the\n    39→// cluster is highly recommended. It's important to keep in mind when configuring\n    40→// this client that the tolerance to skew rate varies inversely to master\n    41→// availability.\n    42→//\n    43→// Larger clusters often have a more lenient SLA for API latency. This should be\n    44→// taken into account when configuring the client. The rate of leader transitions\n    45→// should be monitored and RetryPeriod and LeaseDuration should be increased\n    46→// until the rate is stable and acceptably low. It's important to keep in mind\n    47→// when configuring this client that the tolerance to API latency varies inversely\n    48→// to master availability.\n    49→//\n    50→// DISCLAIMER: this is an alpha API. Wraps TypedDelayingInterface with rate limiter\n   - `AddRateLimited()` calls `AddAfter()` with rate limiter's delay\n\n### Rate Limiting Strategies\n\n**File: `/workspace/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/util/workqueue/default_rate_limiters.go`**\n\nRate limiter interface:\n```\nTypedRateLimiter[T comparable] interface {\n  When(item T) time.Duration      // returns wait duration\n  Forget(item T)                  // clears tracking\n  NumRequeues(item T) int         // returns retry count\n}\n```\n\n**Implementations:**\n\n1. **TypedBucketRateLimiter[T]** (lines 62-77)\n   - Token bucket limiter (golang.org/x/time/rate)\n   - Global rate limiting (10 qps, 100 bucket size)\n   - No per-item tracking\n\n2. **TypedItemExponentialFailureRateLimiter[T]** (lines 84-149)\n   - Per-item exponential backoff: `baseDelay * 2^failures`\n   - Default: 5ms base delay, 1000s max delay\n   - Maps item to failure count\n   - Mutex-protected failure tracking\n\n3. **TypedItemFastSlowRateLimiter[T]** (lines 156-206)\n   - Fast retries for N attempts, then slow retries\n   - Parameters: fast delay, slow delay, max fast attempts\n   - Per-item failure counting\n\n4. **TypedMaxOfRateLimiter[T]** (lines 218-259)\n   - Composes multiple limiters\n   - Returns maximum delay from all limiters\n   - **Default controller limiter** (lines 50-56):\n     - Combines ItemExponential (5ms-1000s) + Bucket (10 qps)\n\n5. **TypedWithMaxWaitRateLimiter[T]** (lines 266-295)\n   - Caps maximum wait time for any limiter\n   - Prevents indefinite delays\n\n### How Rate Limiting Works\n\n1. Item is added via `AddRateLimited(item)`\n2. `rateLimitingType.AddRateLimited()` calls `rateLimiter.When(item)` to get delay\n3. Calls underlying `DelayingInterface.AddAfter(item, delay)`\n4. Item is queued in priority heap to be available after delay\n5. On successful processing, call `Forget(item)` to clear rate limit tracking\n6. If requeued, retry count increments, exponential backoff increases\n\n### Key Files Summary\n\n| File | Purpose |\n|------|---------|\n| `/workspace/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/util/workqueue/queue.go` | Core FIFO queue with dirty/processing sets |\n| `/workspace/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/util/workqueue/delaying_queue.go` | Time-based delay wrapper using heap |\n| `/workspace/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/util/workqueue/rate_limiting_queue.go` | Rate limiter wrapper |\n| `/workspace/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/util/workqueue/default_rate_limiters.go` | Limiter implementations (bucket, exponential, fast-slow, max-of) |\n| `/workspace/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/util/workqueue/metrics.go` | Prometheus metrics collection |\n\n---\n\n## LEADERELECTION PACKAGE ANALYSIS\n\n### Main Components\n\n**File: `/workspace/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/leaderelection/leaderelection.go`**\n\n**1. LeaderElectionConfig** (lines 116-166)\n   - Core configuration:\n     - `Lock rl.Interface` - resource lock (Lease-based)\n     - `LeaseDuration` - how long before non-leader can acquire (default 15s)\n     - `RenewDeadline` - leader's renewal timeout (default 10s)\n     - `RetryPeriod` - retry interval (default 2s)\n     - `Callbacks LeaderCallbacks` - lifecycle callbacks\n     - `ReleaseOnCancel bool` - release lease on context cancel\n     - `Coordinated bool` - enable coordinated leader election (ALPHA)\n\n**2. LeaderCallbacks** (lines 173-185)\n   - `OnStartedLeading(ctx context.Context)` - called when leader acquired\n   - `OnStoppedLeading()` - called when leadership lost\n   - `OnNewLeader(identity string)` - called when leader changes\n\n**3. LeaderElector** (lines 188-206)\n   - Main client with internal state:\n     - `observedRecord` - last seen election record\n     - `reportedLeader` - previously reported leader\n     - `observedTime` - timestamp of observation\n     - `clock` - time wrapper for testing\n     - `observedRecordLock` - RWMutex for thread safety\n\n### How Leader Election Works\n\n**Run() Flow** (lines 211-222):\n1. Call `acquire(ctx)` - loop until leader lease acquired\n2. Once leader: spawn goroutine for `OnStartedLeading` callback\n3. Call `renew(ctx)` - continuously refresh lease until failure\n\n**Acquire Phase** (lines 252-276):\n- Loop calling `tryAcquireOrRenew()` with retry backoff\n- Jitter factor: 1.2 (20% random jitter)\n- Repeat every RetryPeriod until success\n\n**Renew Phase** (lines 279-307):\n- Poll with timeout: `RenewDeadline` / `RetryPeriod`\n- Continuously refresh lease record\n- If renewal fails, exit and call `OnStoppedLeading`\n- If `ReleaseOnCancel=true`, explicitly clear holder identity\n\n**Release** (lines 310-342):\n- Get current lease, verify we're the holder\n- Update with empty holder identity and minimal duration (1 second)\n\n### Resource Lock Interface\n\n**File: `/workspace/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/leaderelection/resourcelock/interface.go`**\n\n**1. Interface** (lines 81-100)\n   ```go\n   type Interface interface {\n     Get(ctx context.Context) (*LeaderElectionRecord, []byte, error)\n     Create(ctx context.Context, ler LeaderElectionRecord) error\n     Update(ctx context.Context, ler LeaderElectionRecord) error\n     RecordEvent(string)\n     Identity() string\n     Describe() string\n   }\n   ```\n\n**2. LeaderElectionRecord** (lines 46-59)\n   - `HolderIdentity` - current lease holder ID\n   - `LeaseDurationSeconds` - seconds lease is held\n   - `AcquireTime` - when leader acquired lease\n   - `RenewTime` - when lease was last renewed\n   - `LeaderTransitions` - count of leadership changes\n   - `Strategy` - coordinated lease strategy (ALPHA)\n   - `PreferredHolder` - preferred leader (ALPHA)\n\n**3. Example: 60s lease / 30s renewal = tolerate 2x time skew\n\n2. **No Fencing**: Does not guarantee exclusive leader access\n   - Relies on timestamps, not authoritative arbitration\n\n3. **Automatic Cleanup**: `release()` zeros out holder identity when stepping down\n\n4. **Thread Safety**: RWMutex protects observed record state\n\n5. **Metrics**: Global factory provides leader-on/leader-off metrics\n\n### Key Files Summary\n\n| File | Purpose |\n|------|---------|\n| `/workspace/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/leaderelection/leaderelection.go` | Main LeaderElector, election logic |\n| `/workspace/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/leaderelection/resourcelock/interface.go` | Lock interface, record definition |\n| `/workspace/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/leaderelection/resourcelock/leaselock.go` | Kubernetes Lease-based lock implementation |\n| `/workspace/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/leaderelection/metrics.go` | Prometheus metrics for leader state |\n| `/workspace/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/leaderelection/healthzadaptor.go` | Health check integration |\n| `/workspace/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/leaderelection/leasecandidate.go` | Coordinated election (ALPHA) |"}],"totalDurationMs":37681,"totalTokens":41516,"totalToolUseCount":14,"usage":{"input_tokens":3,"cache_creation_input_tokens":894,"cache_read_input_tokens":37457,"output_tokens":3162,"server_tool_use":{"web_search_requests":0,"web_fetch_requests":0},"service_tier":"standard","cache_creation":{"ephemeral_1h_input_tokens":894,"ephemeral_5m_input_tokens":0},"inference_geo":"","iterations":[],"speed":"standard"}}}
